Directories whitelisted by BlackByte ransomware\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte optimizes encryption speed based on the targeted file size according to the following rules:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ctable\u003e\r\n\t\u003cthead\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cth\u003eFilesize\u003c/th\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cth\u003eEncryption Algorithm\u003c/th\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/thead\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003eSize \u0026lt;= 5MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003eEncrypt the entire file\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e15MB \u0026gt;= Size \u0026gt; 5MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003eEncrypt the first 1MB and last 1MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e150MB \u0026gt;= Size \u0026gt; 15MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003eEncrypt the first 5MB and last 5MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003eSize \u0026gt; 150MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003eEncrypt the first 50MB and last 50MB\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte renames encrypted files with the extension \u003cem\u003e.blackbyte\u003c/em\u003e. The ransomware creates a \u003cem\u003eDefaultIcon\u003c/em\u003e registry key under \u003cem\u003eHKEY_CLASSES_ROOT\\.blackbyte\u003c/em\u003e that points to an icon file, so that every file that is encrypted will show this icon in Windows explorer. In addition, the registry names \u003cem\u003es1159\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003es2359\u003c/em\u003e are set to \u003cem\u003eBLACKBYTE\u003c/em\u003e under \u003cem\u003eHKEY_CURRENT_USER\\Control Panel\\International\u003c/em\u003e. These registry values control the time format for AM/PM. As a result, Windows will show BLACKBYTE instead of AM/PM as shown below in Figure 2.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"305\" src=\"https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/zrU1_0CAGURBJvvSmsglsIIgAjSvKjzBH0aUOyaRdKV3lmQ2vxTiX6cHxqVXuipfisbKWoJLo9yX3mb9HqJ3iZSfx6FOIp5u6KoqHp0PYPBOq_37P1YNg6BD1DthqqC-OjbilSXV\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 2. BlackByte AM/PM time format modification\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis time format modification is performed by executing the commands:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003ereg add \"HKCU\\Control Panel\\International\" /v s1159 /t REG_SZ /d BLACKBYTE /f\u003c/strong\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003ereg add \"HKCU\\Control Panel\\International\" /v s2359 /t REG_SZ /d BLACKBYTE /f\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eFile Encryption Algorithms (Variant 1)\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte v1 must be executed with the command line argument \u003cstrong\u003e-single\u003c/strong\u003e followed by a SHA256 hash. This hash is combined with a TOR onion URL (e.g., \u003ca href=\"hxxp://7oukjxwkbnwyg7cekudzp66okrchbuubde2j3h6fkpis6izywoj2eqad.%5Donion/\"\u003ehxxp://7oukjxwkbnwyg7cekudzp66okrchbuubde2j3h6fkpis6izywoj2eqad[.]onion/\u003c/a\u003e). The SHA256 hash given as an argument is concatenated to the onion URL to build the URL of the victim ransom portal that is embedded in the ransom note. This URL is substituted in the \u003cstrong\u003e[LINK]\u003c/strong\u003e field of the ransom note template.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhen BlackByte v1 is executed, the malware tries to connect to a hardcoded URL that hosts a file that is involved in the construction of an AES key that is used to encrypt a victim's files. An example URL used for this purpose was \u003ca href=\"hxxps://185.93.6.31/mountain.%5Dpng\"\u003ehxxps://185.93.6[.]31/mountain.png\u003c/a\u003e. The mechanism used to build the AES key is \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/SpiderLabs/BlackByteDecryptor/blob/main/BlackByteDecryptor/Decryptor.cs\"\u003every similar to the C# variant\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter the content of the file mountain.png is downloaded, BlackByte reads the first 16 bytes of the file into a buffer and 24 bytes at the offset 0x410 of the file into another buffer. These 24 bytes are used as key to create and initialize a \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/golang/go/blob/master/src/crypto/des/cipher.go\"\u003eNewTripleDESCipher\u003c/a\u003e object from the Go Cryptographic API. This object is used to decrypt the first 16 bytes of the file mountain.png. The resulting 16-byte buffer will be used as a PBKDF2 password to derive the AES key that will be used to encrypt the victim's files. The BlackByte PBKDF2 algorithm uses SHA1 as the hashing function and 1,000 iterations to derive the AES key. The password is converted to unicode and the unicode string \u003cem\u003eBLACKBYTE_IS_COOL\u003c/em\u003e is used as the salt. The following example Python code can be used to derive the AES key used for file encryption.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"565\" src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/JO2sCc46yEp8VJqkztt9jvvDr5J674fIltI94hoOeD0p4YrwLF1txMdk9ns9Y7ZXXoDTIVmy3OTFAIQ9L68KNZ3MuR5GdY_1a4v5xqST2qpqiMCVQkT1dYg5t7hMKt-rX3O9OFJe\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 3. Python code to decrypt BlackByte v1 files with the file (e.g., mountain.png) downloaded from the C2 server\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eVictim's files are encrypted with AES using CBC mode. The first 16 bytes of the PBKDF2 derived key are used as AES key, and the same 16 bytes are used as the initialization vector (IV). The same AES key is used to encrypt all the files on a victim's machine.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe PBKDF2 password is encrypted with a hardcoded 1,024-bit RSA public key and the resulting RSA-encrypted value is encoded with Base64. This Base64 encoded string is substituted in the \u003cstrong\u003e[KEY]\u003c/strong\u003e field in the ransom note template. The threat actor can decrypt the PBKDF2 password with their corresponding RSA private key, derive the AES key, and thereafter, decrypt the victim's encrypted files. The following is an example RSA public key that was hardcoded in BlackByte:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ctable\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cpre\u003e\r\n-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----\r\nMIGfMA0GCSqGSIb3DQEBAQUAA4GNADCBiQKBgQDUBwECQuQiVGorPYvHrJM11OWV\r\nE1PS8gaBqIAfPaR1rQHUEXu3iX/da/dCtV8Z27/SIA/ZYUNhTyUsX9Snjz8zve90\r\nQAiG1c/BS81WWRax7M7i1rESStVwOaUDAj5w6cz9GwDMGYI+wve9Qyjtw5R6hr5I\r\nqlIEig1Wy1X27vUC2wIDAQAB\r\n-----END PUBLIC KEY-----\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eRansom Note and BlackByte Icon (Variant 1)\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe BlackByte ransom note and an image containing an icon file are stored as Base64 encoded strings in the binary. After the encryption of the victim's files, the ransom note is written to a file named \u003cem\u003eBlackByteRestore.txt\u003c/em\u003e, and the previously mentioned icon file is written to a file named \u003cem\u003eBB.ico\u003c/em\u003e. An example BlackByte v1 ransom note template is shown below in Figure 4. The BlackByte logo uses the extended ASCII characters of the 8-bit \u003ca href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Code_page_437\" title=\"Code page 437\"\u003ecode page 437\u003c/a\u003e to create 3-D block letters.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"173\" src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/svdxEizpcftjmYkB3qcrMlzKW9pHHfHKyX7WIGQusTMu7tEoerSTAgBDzN4Aid-lf-k-OBtM56rvVPcAaG6W0J8vwCyVfPUS34bJ1kVYe4dYSWrqtc5pzTE8fqWm8PQu7uFmYHWK\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 4. Go-based BlackByte v1 ransom note template\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eFile Encryption Algorithms (Variant 2)\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe second variant of BlackByte ransomware does not require a network connection to start encryption. In addition, the ransomware's command-line parameters were modified. BlackByte v2 requires two command line parameters:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003esample.exe \u0026lt;flags\u0026gt; \u0026lt;passphrase\u0026gt;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe first parameter is a flag (e.g., \u003cstrong\u003e-a\u003c/strong\u003e) that controls specific behaviors of the ransomware (e.g., to propagate across a network), while the second parameter is a passphrase (e.g., \u003cstrong\u003e54726956\u003c/strong\u003e) that is verified before file encryption commences. If BlackByte is not provided with any command-line arguments, the ransomware prints out the phrase \u003cem\u003eBlackByte ransomware, 8-th generation, the most destructive of all ransomware products, real natural disaster.\u003c/em\u003e and exits.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte v2 removed the RSA and AES file encryption algorithms from the ransomware. The encryption algorithms were replaced with Curve25519 elliptic curve cryptography for asymmetric encryption and ChaCha for symmetric algorithm. The Curve25519 functions are statically compiled within BlackByte using \u003ca href=\"https://cs.opensource.google/go/x/crypto/+/7b82a4e9:curve25519/curve25519.go\"\u003eGo library code\u003c/a\u003e. BlackByte generates a random 32-byte buffer per file using the Windows API function \u003cem\u003eRtlGenRandom()\u003c/em\u003e. This random value is used as a file's secret key. The file's public key is calculated as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003efile_public_key = Curve25519(file_secret_key, base_point = 0x9)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor's Curve25519 public key is hardcoded in the binary and stored as a Base64 encoded string. For the sample with the SHA256 hash ffc4d94a26ea7bcf48baffd96d33d3c3d53df1bb2c59567f6d04e02e7e2e5aaa, the hardcoded Curve25519 public key was the string:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2BSTzcpdqRW/a2DRT3TiL9lN5INRmmn1lCQWzZhkfQs=\u003c/strong\u003e (d81493cdca5da915bf6b60d14f74e22fd94de483519a69f5942416cd98647d0b)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe shared secret is derived as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eshared_secret = Curve25519(file_secret_key, blackbyte_public_key)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe shared secret is hashed with SHA256 to derive a 32-byte ChaCha encryption key. The ChaCha encryption key is then hashed again with SHA256 to derive the ChaCha nonce (using 12 bytes starting at offset 10). Once the ChaCha key parameters have been derived, they will be used to encrypt the file's content. The encrypted data is written to the file (overwriting the original content). Finally, the victim's 32-byte public key is concatenated to the encrypted content of the file. The BlackByte v2 encryption algorithm is shown below in Figure 5.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"679\" src=\"https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/bXxKZFRFKbevScsHQB-LC_Uuqy99xq26Y8kcVIqoJ9CAy_lZLD1xhhdXGOAay7-hea4NhGzNSrP_lDaLW9xz_BWwRkoJcAREwk78F2DhHChHH_18EDYdZPS28J0O6C8PZjvF727M\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 5. BlackByte v2 file encryption algorithm\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor can use the file's public key together with the threat actor's secret key to recover the shared secret and use it to decrypt the encrypted data as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eshared_secret = Curve25519(blackbyte_secret_key, file_public_key)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe following Python code in Figure 6 can be used to decrypt BlackByte encrypted data from a file that has been encrypted if the threat actor's private key is obtained:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"316\" src=\"https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/lzAioCBczEag8bLRx9tr1nsZzc6LZPLIkpm8gLofNTrl7jhGX4Mf5YsXS7nDwfpRkrx7Ey8wdiS_xunhT0PNl5berPATpImPnvxKIjTNZNzilmGjShmVg-wzG-5N82_cMVSlTUqD\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 6. Python code to decrypt BlackByte v2 files with the threat actor's private key\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte v2 also encrypts the filename after encryption. The encryption is a simple XOR layer with a hardcoded key, followed by Base64 encoding as shown in Figure 7.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Filename Encryption\" data-align=\"left\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/build_enc_filename.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 7. BlackByte v2 filename encryption\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn the analyzed sample, the XOR key was \u003cem\u003efuckyou123\u003c/em\u003e. After a filename has been encrypted, the file is renamed and the .blackbyte extension is concatenated.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThreatLabz has implemented proof-of-concept file decryption code for both BlackByte v1 and v2 \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/threatlabz/tools\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eRansom Note and BlackByte Icon (Variant 2)\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte v2 introduced some improvements to storing the ransom note and icon file. The Base64 encoded blocks for the ransom note and icon file added an XOR-based encryption layer. The XOR key to decrypt the ransom note and icon file is embedded in the ransomware as an obfuscated string. The icon file is written to the victim's \u003cem\u003e%APPDATA%\u003c/em\u003e directory using a randomly generated filename consisting of six upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters (e.g., \u003cem\u003ei2uOJh.ico\u003c/em\u003e).\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte v2 contains a hardcoded TOR onion URL and path for the victim portal rather than relying on the command-line for the path value. BlackByte v2 also added a hardcoded password that is required to access the victim ransom portal. An example password is:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003egkaW_#DD[Aw_JTB@luXpJBdye6eLr@{bx5pHFA)T5FpMYJC]f|@\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe BlackByte v2 ransom note template is shown below in Figure 8. The \u003cstrong\u003e[LINK]\u003c/strong\u003e substring in the ransom note is replaced with the hardcoded BlackByte victim URL and the \u003cstrong\u003e[PASSW]\u003c/strong\u003e substring is replaced with the victim-specific password for the ransom portal.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"400\" src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/sw0iG6WGVHRRM7NQsTjmt1ut60TH-_VPe1Fo57XuYeBOMw9PrvI4lCqyCPueiEXvbAZ-ks3KHXmnS81JvGYaMf31tSL1Eejx-BZ973EctAqjb-u3R6k-9UC0GqhkLQxgMC6lqtp7\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 8. BlackByte v2 ransom note template\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAn example ransom note when populated after file encryption has been performed for BlackByte v2 is shown in Figure 9.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"V2 Ransom Note\" data-align=\"center\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/v2_ransom_note.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 9. BlackByte v2 ransom note\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eA copy of the BlackByte v2 ransom note can be found in the \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/threatlabz/ransomware_notes\"\u003eThreatLabz GitHub repository\u003c/a\u003e. After BlackByte encrypts files, the ransom note is written to each directory, the encrypted files are renamed, and their icons are replaced by the BlackByte icon.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eRansom Portal and Leak Site\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhen a victim accesses the link in the ransom portal, they are instructed to enter the access key from the ransom note as shown in Figure 10.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"V2 Negotiation Channel\" data-align=\"center\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/V2%20negotiation%20channel.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 10. BlackByte victim ransom portal\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter a victim authenticates, they are provided the ransom demand and instructions how to purchase Bitcoin. There is also a live chat feature as shown in Figure 11.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"381\" src=\"https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/DLnqSyLlOWxSxUN3Fk4utE8YDUD_rno8CpTDQzJfxSGSA0FDqA7kFOERJEcvKCPeR5QupY4KX7Bh9Ik2sCq70KdMXfpaey_HnpSkvW8jG5EK7aRlzg8Gf1X5mxrxTDXOk3KhKjqy\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 11. BlackByte ransom negotiation portal\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eVictims are further pressured to pay the ransom, or risk having their data publicly leaked on their TOR hidden service as shown in Figure 12.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"380\" src=\"https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/OejqNE6SEL6jkFTbSPPvFiB2UjZnONXQEujIzfOe70E8Q8bI18riVB8v41-bU4nWYh6Y7nUJq012p9IVLMKCi2w3w3ohorkg9VIu_8Oi3qFu8dsc3ApLjUBLBvGdZK3_2eXVV4e1\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 12. BlackByte victim leak site\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003ePrint Bombing\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition to dropping a ransom note on the victim's machine, the ransomware sends a message to be printed by any connected printers. The printed ransom message is an RTF file with the content shown below:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ctable\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cpre\u003e\r\n{\\rtf1\\ansi\\ansicpg1251\\deff0\\nouicompat\\deflang1049{\\fonttbl{\\f0\\fnil\\fcharset0 system.\\fs22\\par\r\n\\fs56 Your HACKED by BlackByte team.\\par\r\nConnect us to restore your system.\\fs22\\par\r\n\r\n\\pard\\sa200\\sl276\\slmult1\\par\r\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003c/td\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003c/tr\u003e\r\n\t\u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn BlackByte v1, the message is written to the file \u003cem\u003eC:\\Users\\tree.dll\u003c/em\u003e and the following command is executed to print it:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\\\Windows\\\\System32\\\\cmd.exe /c for /l %x in (1,1 ,75) do start wordpad.exe /p C:\\\\Users\\\\tree.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition, a task named \u003cem\u003eTask\u003c/em\u003e is created to print the message every hour:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\schtasks.exe /create /np /sc HOURLY /tn Task /tr \"C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe \r\n/c for /l %x in (1,1,75) do start wordpad.exe /p C:\\Users\\tree.dll\" /st 07:00\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn BlackByte v2, the text of the message is written to a file with a random name consisting of six upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters. The task name is also created randomly consisting of eight upper and lowercase alphabetic and numeric characters. An example task command to print the ransom message is shown below:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eC:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\schtasks.exe /create /np /sc HOURLY /tn 4y77VPNo /tr \"C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe \r\n/c for /l %x in (1,1,75) do start %SystemDrive%\\Program Files\\Windows NT\\Accessories\\WordPad.exe /p \r\nC:\\Users\\1HoWkK.dll\" /st 07:00\u003c/strong\u003e\r\n\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eAnti-Analysis / Anti-Forensics Techniques\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eString Obfuscation\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBoth Go-based BlackByte variants encrypt most strings using a tool similar to \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/andrivet/ADVobfuscator\"\u003eAdvObfuscator\u003c/a\u003e. Each string is decrypted using a unique algorithm with polymorphic code that implements different operations \u003cem\u003exor\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003eaddition\u003c/em\u003e, \u003cem\u003esubtraction\u003c/em\u003e, etc. In the examples below, the encrypted strings in Figure 13 are built and decrypted from arguments on the stack.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg height=\"601\" src=\"https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/nkxvy5vrno0HEs_u0QYAeOlR1kapi-Txh0cokyRwRCAtxw4LSY5D-Jm4TU8OWTyHMvCM5Xesw-ZpLQumZLWDBhiu8tj89kfrpYXKHZspY0pG-DzJd7J2w9K5ezry7_UWcxktHKwV\" alt=\"Image\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 13. BlackByte string obfuscation examples\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003eModified UPX Packer\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition to string obfuscation, BlackByte samples are typically packed with \u003ca href=\"https://upx.github.io/\"\u003eUPX\u003c/a\u003e. In BlackByte v1, all of the samples observed by ThreatLabz were packed with the standard UPX packer and could be unpacked via the command-line parameter \u003cem\u003e-d\u003c/em\u003e. The early samples of BlackByte v2 were also packed with the standard UPX packer. However, the most recent BlackByte samples (since March 2022) are packed with a modified version of UPX. The names of the sections have been renamed from \u003cem\u003eUPX0\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003eUPX1\u003c/em\u003e to \u003cem\u003eBB0\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003eBB1\u003c/em\u003e, respectively. Figure 14 shows an example BlackByte v2 sample with the modified UPX headers.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Altered UPX Header\" data-align=\"center\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/blackbyte/modified_upx_headers.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 14. BlackByte v2 altered UPX header\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eAntivirus Detection\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eDue to BlackByte's anti-analysis features, polymorphic code, and heavy obfuscation many antivirus products have very low detection rates. For example, the BlackByte sample with the SHA256 \u003cem\u003e534f5fbb7669803812781e43c30083e9197d03f97f0d860ae7d9a59c0484ace4\u003c/em\u003e has an antivirus detection rate of 4/61 at the time of publication.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch1\u003eConclusion\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte is a full-featured ransomware family operated by a threat group that continues to breach organizations and demand large ransom amounts. The threat group also performs double extortion attacks by stealing an organization's files and leaking them online if the ransom is not paid. The ransomware code itself is regularly updated to fix bugs, bypass security software, and hinder malware analysis. The encryption algorithms have also been improved to be more secure and prevent file recovery. This demonstrates that the threat group will likely continue to improve the ransomware and remain a significant threat to organizations.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch1\u003eCloud Sandbox Detection\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Cloud Sandbox Detection\"
